<meta name="DC.Title" content="Intertwined signatures of desiccation and drought tolerance in grasses" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1073/pnas.2001928117" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2020-05-05" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="National Academy of Sciences" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="Copyright © 2020 the Author(s). Published by PNAS.. https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/This open access article is distributed under Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-NoDerivatives License 4.0 (CC BY-NC-ND)." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Desiccation tolerance likely evolved independently several times in grasses, providing an ideal comparative system to identify genetic elements controlling this trait. Using comparative genomics, we identified genomic and expression changes distinguishing the desiccation-tolerant grass Eragrostis nindensis from its desiccation-sensitive crop relative Eragrostis tef . We expanded these analyses to include several cereals to identify broadly conserved and divergent patterns during water stress. We found that the distinction between drought and desiccation in grasses is subtle, where genes with essential roles in seed development are broadly expressed under water stress. Thus we propose that seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes of likely ancient origin, with only a few genes uniquely expressed for desiccation tolerance." />
<meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Jeremy Pardo" />
<meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Ching Man Wai" />
<meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Hannah Chay" />
<meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Christine F. Madden" />

<meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Robert VanBuren" />
<meta name="article:published_time" content="2020-05-05" />
<meta name="article:section" content="Biological Sciences" />
<meta name="citation_title" content="Intertwined signatures of desiccation and drought tolerance in grasses" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Grasses are among the most resilient plants, and some can survive prolonged desiccation in semiarid regions with seasonal rainfall. However, the genetic elements that distinguish grasses that are sensitive versus tolerant to extreme drying are largely unknown. Here, we leveraged comparative genomic approaches with the desiccation-tolerant grass &lt;i&gt;Eragrostis nindensis&lt;/i&gt; and the related desiccation-sensitive cereal &lt;i&gt;Eragrostis tef&lt;/i&gt; to identify changes underlying desiccation tolerance. These analyses were extended across C4 grasses and cereals to identify broader evolutionary conservation and divergence. Across diverse genomic datasets, we identified changes in chromatin architecture, methylation, gene duplications, and expression dynamics related to desiccation in &lt;i&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/i&gt;. It was previously hypothesized that transcriptional rewiring of seed desiccation pathways confers vegetative desiccation tolerance. Here, we demonstrate that the majority of seed-dehydration–related genes showed similar expression patterns in leaves of both desiccation-tolerant and -sensitive species. However, we identified a small set of seed-related orthologs with expression specific to desiccation-tolerant species. This supports a broad role for seed-related genes, where many are involved in typical drought responses, with only a small subset of crucial genes specifically induced in desiccation-tolerant plants.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Desiccation tolerance likely evolved independently several times in grasses, providing an ideal comparative system to identify genetic elements controlling this trait. Using comparative genomics, we identified genomic and expression changes distinguishing the desiccation-tolerant grass &lt;i&gt;Eragrostis nindensis&lt;/i&gt; from its desiccation-sensitive crop relative &lt;i&gt;Eragrostis tef&lt;/i&gt;. We expanded these analyses to include several cereals to identify broadly conserved and divergent patterns during water stress. We found that the distinction between drought and desiccation in grasses is subtle, where genes with essential roles in seed development are broadly expressed under water stress. Thus we propose that seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes of likely ancient origin, with only a few genes uniquely expressed for desiccation tolerance.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/18/10079/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Desiccation tolerance likely evolved independently several times in grasses, providing an ideal comparative system to identify genetic elements controlling this trait. Using comparative genomics, we identified genomic and expression changes distinguishing the desiccation-tolerant grass Eragrostis nindensis from its desiccation-sensitive crop relative Eragrostis tef . We expanded these analyses to include several cereals to identify broadly conserved and divergent patterns during water stress. We found that the distinction between drought and desiccation in grasses is subtle, where genes with essential roles in seed development are broadly expressed under water stress. Thus we propose that seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes of likely ancient origin, with only a few genes uniquely expressed for desiccation tolerance." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Desiccation tolerance likely evolved independently several times in grasses, providing an ideal comparative system to identify genetic elements controlling this trait. Using comparative genomics, we identified genomic and expression changes distinguishing the desiccation-tolerant grass Eragrostis nindensis from its desiccation-sensitive crop relative Eragrostis tef . We expanded these analyses to include several cereals to identify broadly conserved and divergent patterns during water stress. We found that the distinction between drought and desiccation in grasses is subtle, where genes with essential roles in seed development are broadly expressed under water stress. Thus we propose that seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes of likely ancient origin, with only a few genes uniquely expressed for desiccation tolerance." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="924269" id="node924269" data-pisa="pnas;117/18/10079" data-pisa-master="pnas;2001928117" data-apath="/pnas/117/18/10079.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-category highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span></div>
  
  
      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Intertwined signatures of desiccation and drought tolerance in grasses</h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="0"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-3419-095X" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Jeremy Pardo</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Ching Man Wai</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="2"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-9734-1930" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Hannah Chay</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="3"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-7386-9063" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Christine F. Madden</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="4"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-6743-583X" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Henk W. M. Hilhorst</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Jill M. Farrant</span>, and <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="6"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-2133-2760" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Robert VanBuren</span></span></div>
  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-metadata"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-journal highwire-cite-metadata">PNAS </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-date highwire-cite-metadata">May 5, 2020 </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-volume highwire-cite-metadata">117 </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-issue highwire-cite-metadata">(18) </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-pages highwire-cite-metadata">10079-10088; </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-papdate highwire-cite-metadata">first published April 23, 2020; </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-doi highwire-cite-metadata"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.2001928117">https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.2001928117</a> </span></div>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-4">Desiccation tolerance likely evolved independently several times in grasses, providing an ideal comparative system to identify genetic elements controlling this trait. Using comparative genomics, we identified genomic and expression changes distinguishing the desiccation-tolerant grass <em>Eragrostis nindensis</em> from its desiccation-sensitive crop relative <em>Eragrostis tef</em>. We expanded these analyses to include several cereals to identify broadly conserved and divergent patterns during water stress. We found that the distinction between drought and desiccation in grasses is subtle, where genes with essential roles in seed development are broadly expressed under water stress. Thus we propose that seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes of likely ancient origin, with only a few genes uniquely expressed for desiccation tolerance.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">Grasses are among the most resilient plants, and some can survive prolonged desiccation in semiarid regions with seasonal rainfall. However, the genetic elements that distinguish grasses that are sensitive versus tolerant to extreme drying are largely unknown. Here, we leveraged comparative genomic approaches with the desiccation-tolerant grass <em>Eragrostis nindensis</em> and the related desiccation-sensitive cereal <em>Eragrostis tef</em> to identify changes underlying desiccation tolerance. These analyses were extended across C4 grasses and cereals to identify broader evolutionary conservation and divergence. Across diverse genomic datasets, we identified changes in chromatin architecture, methylation, gene duplications, and expression dynamics related to desiccation in <em>E. nindensis</em>. It was previously hypothesized that transcriptional rewiring of seed desiccation pathways confers vegetative desiccation tolerance. Here, we demonstrate that the majority of seed-dehydration–related genes showed similar expression patterns in leaves of both desiccation-tolerant and -sensitive species. However, we identified a small set of seed-related orthologs with expression specific to desiccation-tolerant species. This supports a broad role for seed-related genes, where many are involved in typical drought responses, with only a small subset of crucial genes specifically induced in desiccation-tolerant plants.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/drought" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-drought" rel="nofollow">drought</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/grasses" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-grasses" rel="nofollow">grasses</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-evolution" rel="nofollow">evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/desiccation-tolerance" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-desiccation-tolerance" rel="nofollow">desiccation tolerance</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/rewiring" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-rewiring" rel="nofollow">rewiring</a></li></ul><p id="p-6" class="flushleft">Approximately 470 million years ago charophyte green algae emerged from their watery habitat to colonize land (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Exposure to a harsh, dry atmosphere was the main biophysical constraint facing early land plants, resulting in strong selective pressure favoring adaptive mechanisms to prevent dehydration (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). These early protective mechanisms likely served as a foundation for evolving desiccation-tolerant seeds and pollen, which was critical to the success of seed plants (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Although most plants have desiccation-tolerant seeds and pollen, comparatively few can withstand drying of vegetative tissues. Vegetative desiccation tolerance is rare in flowering plants, but it is widespread among other plant lineages (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). The appearance of vegetative desiccation tolerance in phylogenetically distant lineages suggests multiple independent evolutionary origins. In the ecologically and economically important plant family Poaceae, vegetative desiccation tolerance is found within nine separate genera across five different tribes (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>), suggesting that it evolved independently multiple times (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). The current consensus hypothesis is that vegetative desiccation tolerance in angiosperms arose convergently through rewiring of common seed desiccation pathways (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>, <a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>).</p><p id="p-7">Transcriptomic studies on desiccation-tolerant angiosperms consistently show activation of seed-related genes during water-deficit stress (<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a><a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-9"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-10"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-11"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-12"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). However, many of these genes are also highly expressed during water-deficit stress responses in desiccation-sensitive species. The phytohormone abscisic acid (ABA) is critical for seed maturation and drought tolerance where it is hypothesized to play a major role in regulating desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>, <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>) and drought-responsive pathways, respectively (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). Thus, many of the downstream genes that are activated via ABA-dependent mechanisms are expressed broadly during seed development and in leaf tissues under mild and severe water deficit (desiccation). Accumulation of osmoprotectants and activation of reactive oxygen species quenching mechanisms are also shared responses between these conditions. Thus, it is important to distinguish desiccation-tolerance responses from broader water-deficit stress responses.</p><p id="p-8">While numerous transcriptomic studies of desiccation-tolerant plants have been published, few previous studies have compared the responses of desiccation-sensitive and desiccation-tolerant plants with a close phylogenetic relationship. Previous work comparing the eudicot species <em>Lindernia brevidens</em> (desiccation tolerant) and <em>Lindernia subracemosa</em> (desiccation sensitive) provided some insight into genes that are involved in desiccation tolerance and not drought (<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). However, the Linderniaceae family is of little economic importance and is only distantly related to any crop plants, making it difficult to translate these discoveries. Cereals from the grass family (Poaceae) are the most important crops for global food security, and our current study with desiccation-tolerant Poaceae species is likely more readily translatable. The Chloridoideae subfamily of grasses contains the majority of desiccation-tolerant species with multiple independent phylogenetic origins. Chloridoideae also contains the cereals finger millet (<em>Eleusine coracana</em>) and tef (<em>Eragrostis tef</em>), which are widely consumed in semiarid regions of Eastern Africa and Asia. To our knowledge, <em>Eragrostis</em> is the only genus with both desiccation-tolerant and cereal crop species. Thus, <em>Eragrostis</em> and, more broadly, the Chloridoideae subfamily, are ideal systems in which to identify genes involved in desiccation tolerance that are potential targets for improving drought resilience in crops.</p><p id="p-9">Chromosome-scale genome assemblies of the Chloridoideae grasses <em>E. tef</em> (desiccation sensitive) and <em>Oropetium thomaeum</em> (desiccation tolerant) were recently completed (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). Here, we sequenced the desiccation-tolerant grass <em>Eragrostis nindensis</em> and performed detailed comparative genomics within Chloridoideae and across the grass family to search for patterns of convergence in the evolution of desiccation tolerance. We conducted parallel dehydration experiments with <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> using matched physiological sampling points to identify signatures that distinguish water stress and desiccation responses. We leveraged seed expression data of <em>E. tef</em> and other grass species to test if desiccation tolerance in grasses arose through co-option of seed dehydration pathways. Together, our results identify similar signatures of water-deficit (drought) stress and desiccation responses with a smaller number of genomic features and expression changes unique to desiccation-tolerant grasses. We propose a model where seeds and leaves share common sets of coregulated genes under water deficit with only a few genes uniquely involved in desiccation responses.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Evolution of Chloridoid Grasses.</h3><p id="p-10">Comparative systems with phylogenetically similar desiccation-sensitive and -tolerant species are a powerful tool to elucidate the genetic basis of desiccation tolerance. Only one previous study has conducted genome-wide comparisons between a desiccation-sensitive and -tolerant angiosperm (<a id="xref-ref-13-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), and no such systems are currently available for the grasses. We assembled a draft genome of the desiccation-tolerant grass <em>E. nindensis</em> and compared it to the recently sequenced <em>E. tef</em> genome (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>) to distinguish genetic elements associated with desiccation tolerance from those more broadly linked with drought response. Similar to most (∼90%) of chloridoid grasses, <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> are polyploid, and they have the same karotype (2n = 4× = 40) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). We utilized a single-molecule real-time sequencing approach to overcome assembly issues related to tetraploidy and heterozygosity in <em>E. nindensis</em>. In total, we generated 64 Gb of PacBio data representing 63× coverage of the 1.0-Gb <em>E. nindensis</em> genome. We used Canu with parameters optimized to assemble all haplotypes yielding an initial assembly of roughly twice the haploid genome size (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). We then applied the pseudohaploid algorithm to filter out redundant haplotypes from the assembly (see <a id="xref-sec-8-1" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a> for details) (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). This filtering approach produced a total haploid assembly of 986 Mb across 4,368 contigs with an N50 of 520 kb, hereafter referred to as <em>E. nindensis</em> V2.1. We used the MAKER-P pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>) to annotate the <em>E. nindensis</em> genome, and after filtering the annotation based on orthology, pfam domains, and expression, we identified a set of 107,683 high-confidence genes (<a id="xref-sec-8-2" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>).</p><p id="p-11">The three chrloridoid grass genomes of <em>E. nindensis</em>, <em>E. tef</em>, and <em>O. thomeaum</em> have largely conserved gene content and order (synteny) (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). A high proportion of the <em>E. tef</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em> genomes matched the expected 2:2 ratio of syntenic gene blocks given their tetraploidy, but a substantial portion of the <em>E. nindensis</em> genome has three or four blocks for each homeologous region of <em>E. tef</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2<em>B</em></a>). <em>E. nindensis</em> and the diploid <em>O. thomaeum</em> have 2:1 synteny with similarly duplicated blocks of three or more in some regions (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2<em>A</em></a>). This syntenic pattern is likely a result of assembling multiple haplotypes for each homeologous region in <em>E. nindensis</em> and a single haplotype for <em>E. tef</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>. This is supported by the distribution of synonymous substitutions (Ks) between orthologous genes in <em>E. nindensis</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). We observed a strong peak of Ks corresponding to haplotype sequences and homeologous gene pairs. The high degree of collinearity and shared gene content between the three sequenced Chloridoideae grasses allowed us to identify shared and unique genomic signatures of desiccation tolerance.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Collinearity of Chloridoideae grasses. Microsynteny of collinear regions between allotetraploid E. nindensis and E. tef and diploid O. thomaeum is shown. Genes in the forward and reverse orientation are shown in gold and blue, respectively, and syntenic gene pairs are connected by gray lines." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Collinearity of Chloridoideae grasses. Genes in the forward and reverse orientation are shown in gold and blue, respectively, and syntenic gene pairs are connected by gray lines.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="334"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="334"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Collinearity of Chloridoideae grasses. Microsynteny of collinear regions between allotetraploid <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> and diploid <em>O. thomaeum</em> is shown. Genes in the forward and reverse orientation are shown in gold and blue, respectively, and syntenic gene pairs are connected by gray lines.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Water-Deficit Responses between <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>.</h3><p id="p-13">We conducted parallel dehydration time courses for <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> under comparable conditions to distinguish drought- and desiccation-associated responses (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank">Dataset S1</a>). Water-deficit treatment group plants were dried in a controlled manner until they reached mild water-deficit stress [75.8 and 60.4% relative water content (RWC), respectively]. We then sampled during moderate and severe dehydration stress for both species, with only <em>E. nindensis</em> being sampled after prolonged desiccation, and on recovery, since <em>E. tef</em> does not survive below ∼30% RWC (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>). We collected tissue for leaf RNA-Sequencing (RNA-Seq) data in parallel with physiological data for eight time points in <em>E. nindensis</em> and three for <em>E. tef</em>. In <em>E. nindensis</em>, these corresponded to the fully hydrated control (well watered [WW], RWC 90.36%) moderate dehydration (D1, RWC 67.57%), severe dehydration (D2, 15.18% RWC) and desiccated (D3, 14.28% RWC) and on rehydration for 0 h (14.56% RWC), 12 h (80.82% RWC), 24 h (93.16% RWC), and 48 h (86.05% RWC). In <em>E. tef</em>, these included the WW control (93.89% RWC), D1 (32.68% RWC), and D2 (16.32% RWC). We collected <em>E. tef</em> samples below the minimum RWC from which they can recover in order to capture their response to a lethal water-deficit stress; however, we did not collect samples from <em>E. tef</em> plants maintained at this low RWC level for a longer period of time since the leaves would already have senesced. Nocturnal upticks in RWC across the drying time courses are associated with nighttime hydraulic redistribution (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative parallel dehydration experiments in Eragrostis grasses. (A) Parallel drought/desiccation time course in E. tef (Left) and E. nindensis (Right). E. nindensis plants at 24 and 48 h post rehydration are also shown. E. tef droughted plants are shown next to a well-watered plant in each image. Each pot contains individual E. tef and E. nindensis, and these plants were collected separatley from the timecourses depicted in B. (B) Relative water content changes across the desiccation and rehydration experiments for E. tef (blue) and E. nindensis (orange) with 95% confidence intervals plotted for each time point. The sampling points for RNA-seq are labeled WW (well watered), D1, D2, and D3 (first, second, and third drought-sampling point, respectively), and 0, 12, 24, and 48 h post rehydration)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative parallel dehydration experiments in Eragrostis grasses. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Parallel drought/desiccation time course in &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;Left&lt;/em&gt;) and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;Right&lt;/em&gt;). &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; plants at 24 and 48 h post rehydration are also shown. &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; droughted plants are shown next to a well-watered plant in each image. Each pot contains individual &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt;, and these plants were collected separatley from the timecourses depicted in &lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Relative water content changes across the desiccation and rehydration experiments for &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; (blue) and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; (orange) with 95% confidence intervals plotted for each time point. The sampling points for RNA-seq are labeled WW (well watered), D1, D2, and D3 (first, second, and third drought-sampling point, respectively), and 0, 12, 24, and 48 h post rehydration).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F2.medium.gif" width="260" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F2.medium.gif" width="260" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924406" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-14" class="first-child">Comparative parallel dehydration experiments in Eragrostis grasses. (<em>A</em>) Parallel drought/desiccation time course in <em>E. tef</em> (<em>Left</em>) and <em>E. nindensis</em> (<em>Right</em>). <em>E. nindensis</em> plants at 24 and 48 h post rehydration are also shown. <em>E. tef</em> droughted plants are shown next to a well-watered plant in each image. Each pot contains individual <em>E. tef</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em>, and these plants were collected separatley from the timecourses depicted in <em>B</em>. (<em>B</em>) Relative water content changes across the desiccation and rehydration experiments for <em>E. tef</em> (blue) and <em>E. nindensis</em> (orange) with 95% confidence intervals plotted for each time point. The sampling points for RNA-seq are labeled WW (well watered), D1, D2, and D3 (first, second, and third drought-sampling point, respectively), and 0, 12, 24, and 48 h post rehydration).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-15">We tracked electrolyte leakage across the desiccation time course as a proxy for membrane damage and cell death. In both <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>, the percentage of total leakage increased significantly during the most severe dehydration and desiccation time points (<em>E. nindensis</em> D2, D3, R0, and <em>E. tef</em> D2) compared to the well-watered control (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>). During the rehydration experiment, the electrolyte leakage percentage decreased back to the well-watered level by 12 h post rehydration in <em>E. nindensis</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>), suggesting that, while some membrane reorganization or damage might occur during desiccation, the damage is repaired on rehydration. Similar data have been reported previously for both species with the percentage of leakage returning to a predesiccated state in <em>E. nindens</em>, but with levels further increasing on rehydration for <em>E. teff</em> plants that have been dehydrated below 30 to 40% RWC (<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>).</p><p id="p-16">Across the time courses, 26,275 genes (24.4% of high-confidence genes) in <em>E. nindensis</em> were differentially expressed between well-watered leaves and at least one drought or rehydration time point. Of these, 7,504 increased in transcript abundance (hereafter up-regulated) and 19,506 decreased in transcript abundance (hereafter down-regulated). Down-regulated genes under drought in <em>E. nindensis</em> were significantly enriched in numerous gene ontology (GO) terms related to photosynthesis, while up-regulated genes were significantly enriched in abiotic stress-response–related terms (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank">Dataset S2</a>). We compared GO enrichment between <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> by grouping GO terms into categories of related terms (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix, Supplemental Materials and Methods</em></a>). In <em>E. nindensis</em>, two seed-related terms were enriched while one seed-related term was enriched in <em>E. tef</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S6 and S7</a>, and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank">Dataset S3</a>). Interestingly, six light response terms were enriched in <em>E. nindensis</em> but no light response terms were enriched in <em>E. tef.</em> Genes up-regulated during rehydration were significantly enriched in GO terms related to photosynthesis and, specifically, in photoprotection and regulation of photomorphogenesis (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank">Dataset S4</a>).</p><p id="p-17">Because of the polyploid nature of <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>, we compared expression patterns between the two species using sets of “syntenic orthogroups” (conserved collinear genes) rather than individual gene pairs. Syntenic orthogroups were defined using the MCscan algorithm in blocks of 2:4 corresponding to the allotetraploidy in <em>E. tef</em> and allotetraploidy plus heterozygosity in <em>E. nindensis</em>. Of the conserved syntenic gene groups between <em>E. tef</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em>, 5,600 and 6,199 syntenic groups were up-regulated during the first two water-stress time points (D1, D2) in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). The majority of these syntenic orthogroups (3,254) were up-regulated in both species (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>), supporting a broad conservation of drought responses. To further examine the degree of conservation between <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>, we compared the maximum expression in each syntenic group for the well-watered, D1, and D2 time points. Expression was significantly correlated between the two species (F-test, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001; Pearson’s <em>r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.54), suggesting that similar pathways are involved in response to water deficit (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a>). A subset of orthogroups were uniquely expressed in each species, and across the comparable time points, 2,346 syntenic orthogroups were uniquely up-regulated in <em>E. nindensis</em> (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>), and 2,945 syntenic orthogroups were uniquely up-regulated in <em>E. tef</em>. Candidate genes that confer vegetative desiccation tolerance are most likely to come from the gene set unique to <em>E. nindensis</em>.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Shared and unique desiccation-associated expression changes. (A) Heatmap of shared and unique syntenic orthogroups up-regulated in drought/desiccation time points of E. tef and E. nindensis. D1 and D2 represent the first and second drought time point for E. nindensis and E. tef, respectively. (B) Venn diagram of shared and uniquely up-regulated syntenic orthogroups. (C) Correlation of expression, measured as log2 transcripts per million (TPM), between the syntenic orthogroups of E. tef and E. nindensis where syntenic orthogroups with similar up-regulation are shown in brown, and uniquely up-regulated orthogroups in E. nindensis are shown in blue." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Shared and unique desiccation-associated expression changes. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Heatmap of shared and unique syntenic orthogroups up-regulated in drought/desiccation time points of &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt;. D1 and D2 represent the first and second drought time point for &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt;, respectively. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Venn diagram of shared and uniquely up-regulated syntenic orthogroups. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Correlation of expression, measured as log&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; transcripts per million (TPM), between the syntenic orthogroups of &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; where syntenic orthogroups with similar up-regulation are shown in brown, and uniquely up-regulated orthogroups in &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; are shown in blue.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="254"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="254"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924447" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-18" class="first-child">Shared and unique desiccation-associated expression changes. (<em>A</em>) Heatmap of shared and unique syntenic orthogroups up-regulated in drought/desiccation time points of <em>E. tef</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em>. D1 and D2 represent the first and second drought time point for <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em>, respectively. (<em>B</em>) Venn diagram of shared and uniquely up-regulated syntenic orthogroups. (<em>C</em>) Correlation of expression, measured as log<sub>2</sub> transcripts per million (TPM), between the syntenic orthogroups of <em>E. tef</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em> where syntenic orthogroups with similar up-regulation are shown in brown, and uniquely up-regulated orthogroups in <em>E. nindensis</em> are shown in blue.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Induction of “Seed-Related Pathways” during Dehydration and Desiccation.</h3><p id="p-19">The long-standing hypothesis that vegetative desiccation tolerance evolved from the rewiring of seed development pathways has been supported by several recent genome-scale analyses (<a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-7-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>, <a id="xref-ref-8-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). We tested this hypothesis in <em>E. nindensis</em> by examining whether genes with typically seed-specific expression are induced during vegetative desiccation. We then used a comparative approach to test if these seed- related genes are more broadly expressed during dehydration in <em>E. tef</em> and other cereals. We created a list of “seed-related” genes in grasses through comparing seed and leaf expression datasets from four cereals (<em>E. tef</em>, <em>Oryza sativa</em>, <em>Zea mays</em>, and <em>Sorghum bicolor</em>) and identifying genes with high expression in seeds relative to well-watered leaves (<a id="xref-sec-8-3" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>). To facilitate cross-species comparisons, we identified pairwise syntenic orthologs between each of the six grass species with comparative expression datasets (<em>E. nindensis</em>, <em>E. tef</em>, <em>O. thomaeum</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>Z. mays</em>, and <em>S. bicolor</em>), herein referred to as syntelog groups (<a id="xref-sec-8-4" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>). In total, we identified 640 syntelog groups with conserved induction in seeds compared to well-watered leaves. We clustered the syntelog groups into 386 orthogroups; of these, we found that 189 seed-related orthogroups were up-regulated in <em>E. nindensis</em> leaves in at least one of the stages of dehydration (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6<em>B</em></a>). Using a permutation test, we determined that this number was significantly more than expected by chance (permutation test: <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001), suggesting that expression of seed-related genes is critical for drought response in <em>E. nindensis</em> leaves (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>).</p><p id="p-20">We then compared the <em>E. nindensis</em> expression data with reanalyzed well-watered and drought-stressed leaf expression data from the same four grass species to determine whether the observed enrichment of seed-related genes is unique to desiccation-tolerant plants or if it represents a more broadly shared water-deficit stress response. We counted the number of seed-related syntegroups up-regulated in leaves during water deficit in each of the six species. <em>E. tef</em> had the greatest number of seed-related genes up-regulated in dehydrated leaves, followed by <em>O. thomaeum</em>, <em>E. nindensis</em>, and <em>O. sativa</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>). <em>S. bicolor</em> and <em>Z. mays</em> had fewer seed-related genes up-regulated in water-stressed leaves compared to the other four species. The number and severity of water-deficit stress time points differed between species, limiting our power to draw conclusions about differences in expression of seed-related genes during water deficit between the species. However, these data do suggest that the number of such genes up-regulated during water-deficit stress in the desiccation-tolerant grasses <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> is similar to the number observed during severe water-deficit conditions in desiccation-sensitive grasses.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Induction of seed pathways during drought in grasses. (A) Bar graph of seed-related gene expression in drought-treated leaf tissue of the desiccation-tolerant grasses O. thomaeum and E. nindensis (purple) and desiccation-sensitive cereals E. tef, S. bicolor, Z. mays, and O. sativa (gold). (B) Correlation of leaf RWC vs. induced seed-related pathways for drought-treated desiccation-tolerant (purple) and -sensitive (gold) species." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Induction of seed pathways during drought in grasses. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Bar graph of seed-related gene expression in drought-treated leaf tissue of the desiccation-tolerant grasses &lt;em&gt;O. thomaeum&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; (purple) and desiccation-sensitive cereals &lt;em&gt;E. tef&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;S. bicolor&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Z. mays&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;O. sativa&lt;/em&gt; (gold). (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Correlation of leaf RWC vs. induced seed-related pathways for drought-treated desiccation-tolerant (purple) and -sensitive (gold) species.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="161"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 4." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="161"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924470" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">Induction of seed pathways during drought in grasses. (<em>A</em>) Bar graph of seed-related gene expression in drought-treated leaf tissue of the desiccation-tolerant grasses <em>O. thomaeum</em> and <em>E. nindensis</em> (purple) and desiccation-sensitive cereals <em>E. tef</em>, <em>S. bicolor</em>, <em>Z. mays</em>, and <em>O. sativa</em> (gold). (<em>B</em>) Correlation of leaf RWC vs. induced seed-related pathways for drought-treated desiccation-tolerant (purple) and -sensitive (gold) species.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-22">For the four species for which RWC data were available for the sampled time points (<em>E. nindensis</em>, <em>E. tef</em>, <em>O. thomaeum</em>, and <em>S. bicolor</em>), we compared the number of seed-related syntelog groups up-regulated during each drought time point with the mean RWC of all replicates from that time point (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a>). We found that RWC was a significant predictor of the number of seed-related syntelogs up-regulated in leaves during drought (F-test, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001), and RWC also explained a substantial amount of the variation in the number of seed-related syntelogs up-regulated in leaves during drought (Pearson’s, <em>r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.72). Conversely, whether a sample was derived from a desiccation-tolerant or -sensitive plant did not significantly predict the number of seed-related syntelog groups up-regulated in leaves during drought (F-test, <em>P</em> = 0.53). This model did not explain much of the observed variation (<em>r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.041). The spread of the model residuals was also much larger for this desiccation tolerance model as compared with the RWC model, indicating a better fit for the RWC model (F-test, <em>P</em> = 0.001) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8</a>). While this analysis is somewhat limited by the paucity of RWC measurements, and diverse time points over which plants were dried out, we can tentatively conclude that more seed-related genes are up-regulated at lower RWC in grasses regardless of whether the species possesses desiccation tolerance.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Unique Components of Desiccation Tolerance in Grasses.</h3><p id="p-23">Comparisons of drought-induced expression across grasses suggest that seed pathways are not uniquely induced in resurrection plants but instead represent a conserved response to water deficit. Using this comparative framework, we searched for unique patterns that distinguish tolerant and sensitive species. <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> represent separate tribes and likely independent origins of desiccation tolerance within the Chloridoideae subfamily. These species also utilize different photoprotective strategies under desiccation where <em>E. nindensis</em> largely degrades and <em>O. thomaeum</em> retains chlorophyll. We distinguished conserved expression patterns indicative of convergent evolution from species-specific patterns reflecting differences in desiccation strategies. We used <em>k</em>-means clustering to group <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> genes based on a curve fit to their expression pattern during separate desiccation and rehydration time series (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5<em>A</em></a>). For each resulting cluster, we counted the number of <em>O. thomaeum</em> genes with at least one coclustering <em>E. nindenis</em> syntenic ortholog. Genes in clusters that on average contained genes more strongly up-regulated during desiccation were more likely to cocluster with their <em>E. nindensis</em> syntenic orthologs. In addition, the <em>x</em><sup>2</sup> coefficient for syntenic ortholog pairs in both species were significantly correlated (Pearson’s, <em>r</em> = 0.53; F-test, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001), suggesting overall conservation of expression patterns between the two species (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5<em>B</em></a>). In particular, expression patterns of seed-related genes were significantly more strongly correlated (Pearson’s <em>r</em> = 0.69), <em>t</em> test for sample correlation coefficient (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.001) than either all pairs (Pearson’s <em>r</em> = 0.53) or all syntenic pairs up-regulated in both species (Pearson’s <em>r</em> = 0.55). This pattern of conservation suggests that seed-related genes may have similar roles, and possibly similar regulation, during dehydration and rehydration in both <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>.</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative network dynamics across desiccation-tolerant grasses. (A) K-mean clusters for the E. nindensis and O. thomaeum desiccation and rehydration time courses. (B) Cluster coefficient of expression patterns of syntenic orthogroups." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative network dynamics across desiccation-tolerant grasses. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) K-mean clusters for the &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;O. thomaeum&lt;/em&gt; desiccation and rehydration time courses. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Cluster coefficient of expression patterns of syntenic orthogroups.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="173"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 5." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="173"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924293" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 5.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">Comparative network dynamics across desiccation-tolerant grasses. (<em>A</em>) K-mean clusters for the <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> desiccation and rehydration time courses. (<em>B</em>) Cluster coefficient of expression patterns of syntenic orthogroups.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">We identified a set of 239 syntenic orthogroups which were up-regulated under water-deficit stress in both <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> but not in any of the four desiccation-sensitive species examined. Based on a permutation test, an overlap of 239 genes is significantly more than expected by random chance (permutation test: <em>P</em> &lt; 0.001) (<a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>). Among the conserved desiccation-associated genes in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> are a wide array of transcription factors (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>) including orthologs of <em>Arabidopsis</em> DOG1 and HY5, which regulate seed dormancy and photomorphogenesis, respectively. The ABA-dependent transcription factor ABI3 is important for controlling seed development and is thought to be critical for vegetative desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-7-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). However, the set of conserved up-regulated transcription factors does not include orthologs of <em>Arabidopsis</em> ABI3 or any other LAFL transcription factors responsible for regulating seed dormancy. Of the 23 orthogroups containing target genes of the ABI3 regulon in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>), 16 and 20 contained at least one gene which was up-regulated during desiccation in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>, respectively. However, 16 of the 23 orthogroups were also up-regulated during water-deficit stress in <em>E. tef</em>, and 12 ABI3-regulated orthogroups were up-regulated during water stress in <em>O. sativa</em>. We identified only one ABI3-regulated orthogroup (OG0002708) containing cupin seed storage proteins that is uniquely up-regulated in only the desiccation-tolerant grasses. Furthermore, of the 239 genes with vegetative expression unique to the two desiccation-tolerant species, only 14 were from our list of seed-related genes, which is not more than expected by random chance (permutation test: <em>P</em> = 0.37). Taken together, this suggests that much of the ABI3 regulon, and seed-related genes more broadly, are induced in leaves during both dehydration and desiccation, implying that expression of seed-related genes alone is insufficient to confer desiccation tolerance.</p><p id="p-26">Late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) proteins are believed to play an important role in protecting cellular components from damage during desiccation (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>). It is likely that certain LEA proteins (or groups of LEAs) play an essential and conserved role in vegetative desiccation tolerance, but some are also more broadly involved in drought response pathways among desiccation-sensitive plants (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). We identified LEA genes belonging to the eight LEA subfamilies in the genomes of <em>E. nindensis</em>, <em>E. tef</em>, and <em>O. thomaeum</em> using PFAM domains (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S9</a>). We found no evidence of expansion of any LEA subfamilies in <em>E. nindensis</em> relative to <em>E. tef</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S9 and S10</a>). The LEA5 and LEA6 (also referred to as LEA18) subfamilies were uniquely induced during desiccation and rehydration in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>, but neither subfamily showed increased expression during water stress in <em>E. tef.</em> The remaining LEA subfamilies showed similar patterns across all three species (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S10</a>).</p><p id="p-27">All previously sequenced resurrection plant genomes have massive tandem arrays of early light-induced proteins (ELIPs) to protect against photooxidative damage during prolonged desiccation (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Consistent with this pattern, the <em>E. nindensis</em> genome has 27 ELIPs, and most are found in large tandem arrays (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11<em>A</em></a>). The ELIPs in <em>E. nindensis</em> are nonsyntenic to the 22 orthologs in <em>O. thomaeum</em> and the 5 orthologs in <em>E. tef</em>, suggesting that they translocated and duplicated after the divergence of these grasses. The nonsyntenic nature of the ELIP tandem arrays in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> supports an independent origin of desiccation tolerance, as distinct ELIPs were duplicated in each species. ELIPs are induced under water stress with 23 of the 27 up-regulated during desiccation or rehydration in <em>E. nindensis</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11<em>B</em></a>). ELIPs are most highly expressed 12 and 24 h post rehydration, contrasting with most other species where ELIPs are highest in desiccated tissues (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Unlike <em>O. thomaeum</em>, <em>E. nindensis</em> largely degrades its chlorophyll and dismantles thyllakoids (a strategy termed poikilochlorophylly) during desiccation, with the thyllakoids and chlorophyll being reconstituted upon rehydration. In turn, this results in a comparatively slow post-rehydration recovery.</p><p id="p-28">Chlorophyll degradation is catalyzed by chlorophyllase enzymes, and we observed different expression patterns in <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>, reflecting alternate strategies of chlorophyll degradation or retention during desiccation (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>). An <em>E. nindensis</em> chlorophyllase (En_0076685) was up-regulated 3.5-fold under desiccation but had no change in expression during any other time point. The syntenic ortholog in <em>O. thomaeum</em> (Ot_Chr2_06331) was not up-regulated during any desiccation time point, but was slightly up-regulated 48 h post rehydration. The two <em>E. tef</em> chlorophyllases (Et_2A_015704 and Et_2B_019834) are up-regulated in seeds but not in leaves, suggesting that desiccation-associated expression of chlorophyllase may be specific to chlorophyll-degrading resurrection plants. Senescence-related chlorophyll degradation is catalyzed by pheophytinase (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>, <a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>) which is up-regulated in all three species, suggesting that pheophytinase activity is a more general drought response (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Chromatin Dynamics and Epigenetic Changes during Desiccation.</h3><p id="p-29">Alterations of histone modifications and DNA methylation are correlated with stress-induced gene expression, and these processes are integral for many stress responses (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). Histone modifications are also important for regulating seed dormancy possibly through a thermal-sensing role (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). It was previously suggested that chromatin modifications may be partly responsible for gene regulatory changes required for desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>, <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). We surveyed methylation changes and chromatin dynamics in well-watered and desiccated <em>E. nindensis</em> leaves using Bisulfite-seq and ChIP-seq with a histone modification associated with open chromatin (H3K4me3). H3K4me3 is correlated with active transcription, and these histone marks accumulate immediately upstream of the transcriptional start site of actively transcribed genes (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). We identified regions with differential binding of H3K4me3 antibody between well-watered <em>E. nindensis</em> leaves and desiccated leaves from the D3 time point. Across the three replicates of well-watered leaves, we identified 25,754 H3K4me3 peaks with significant enriched coverage (Wald test q &lt; 0.05) over the input control (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>). The D3 samples contained 47,312 peaks, and 15,832 peaks overlapped by at least one base with the well-watered peaks. Despite the large number of unique peaks in each condition, only 3,757 peaks had significantly greater binding in D3 compared to WW (Wald test q &lt; 0.05), and 949 peaks had significantly more binding in WW compared to D3 (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>C</em></a>). We identified the closest gene to each of these differentially bound peaks and tested for enrichment of genes with up- or down-regulated expression in D3 compared with WW. We found significant enrichment of both up- and down-regulated genes among genes proximal to the peaks with increased binding in D3 (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>B</em></a>). Only genes down-regulated in D3 compared to WW were enriched for H3K4me3 peaks with increased binding in the WW samples. The significant number of altered H3K4me3 histone modifications and overlap with differentially expressed genes suggests that chromatin dynamics play a central role in desiccation tolerance.</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Changes in histone modifications associated with desiccation in E. nindensis. (A) Venn diagram of overlapping H3K4me3 peaks between well-watered and desiccated samples. (B) Enrichment of H3K4me3 peaks and up-regulated genes during desiccation. The expected background distribution is shown in light blue or light red, and the observed is shown in red and blue for up-regulated and down-regulated genes, respectively. (C) Heatmap of fold enrichment of peaks at the transcriptional start site of genes. The color scale represents log2 enrichment values over the input." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Changes in histone modifications associated with desiccation in &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Venn diagram of overlapping H3K4me3 peaks between well-watered and desiccated samples. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Enrichment of H3K4me3 peaks and up-regulated genes during desiccation. The expected background distribution is shown in light blue or light red, and the observed is shown in red and blue for up-regulated and down-regulated genes, respectively. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Heatmap of fold enrichment of peaks at the transcriptional start site of genes. The color scale represents log2 enrichment values over the input.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F6.medium.gif" width="360" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F6.medium.gif" width="360" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924481" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-30" class="first-child">Changes in histone modifications associated with desiccation in <em>E. nindensis</em>. (<em>A</em>) Venn diagram of overlapping H3K4me3 peaks between well-watered and desiccated samples. (<em>B</em>) Enrichment of H3K4me3 peaks and up-regulated genes during desiccation. The expected background distribution is shown in light blue or light red, and the observed is shown in red and blue for up-regulated and down-regulated genes, respectively. (<em>C</em>) Heatmap of fold enrichment of peaks at the transcriptional start site of genes. The color scale represents log2 enrichment values over the input.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-31">We surveyed changes in DNA methylation in desiccated (D3), rehydrated, and well-watered leaf tissue. Similar to other plants, <em>E. nindensis</em> has low levels of CHH methylation across the genome, and moderate levels of CpG and CHG methylation (<a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7<em>A</em></a>). There was no global difference in methylation levels across the surveyed drought and rehydration time points. However, methylation levels upstream, downstream, and within the gene body varied across the three time points (<a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7<em>B</em></a>). CpG and CHH gene body methylation was lower in desiccated and rehydrated leaf tissue compared to well-watered leaf tissue. Interestingly, CHG gene body methylation was higher in rehydrated leaf tissue compared to well-watered and desiccated leaf tissue, with no reduction in methylation level around the upstream transcriptional start site or downstream transcriptional termination site (<a id="xref-fig-7-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7<em>B</em></a>). This general pattern is consistent with stress-induced hypomethylation and transcriptional reprogramming (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>).</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Desiccation-induced changes in DNA methylation in E. nindensis. (A) Global patterns of CHH, CpG, and CHG methylation across the genome for WW, desiccated (D3), and 24-h postrehydration (R24) leaf samples. (B) Gene body methylation for the three methylation contexts in the surveyed samples. Methylation is plotted in a rolling window for all genes in the upstream (transcriptional start site: TSS), downstream (transcriptional termination site: TTS), and body of genes." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-736421303" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Desiccation-induced changes in DNA methylation in &lt;em&gt;E. nindensis&lt;/em&gt;.
