(&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Gene body methylation for the three methylation contexts in the surveyed samples. Methylation is plotted in a rolling window for all genes in the upstream (transcriptional start site: TSS), downstream (transcriptional termination site: TTS), and body of genes.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 7." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="291"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 7." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="291"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F7.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 7." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/18/10079/F7.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/924357" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 7.</span> <p id="p-32" class="first-child">Desiccation-induced changes in DNA methylation in <em>E. nindensis</em>. (<em>A</em>) Global patterns of CHH, CpG, and CHG methylation across the genome for WW, desiccated (D3), and 24-h postrehydration (R24) leaf samples. (<em>B</em>) Gene body methylation for the three methylation contexts in the surveyed samples. Methylation is plotted in a rolling window for all genes in the upstream (transcriptional start site: TSS), downstream (transcriptional termination site: TTS), and body of genes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-33" class="flushleft">Desiccation tolerance has evolved recurrently across plants, animals, and microbes as a common adaptation to water-limited environments. Surviving extreme drying requires the coordinated deployment of complex processes to prevent oxidative damage and protect the macromolecules and membranes of cells. Some core elements of desiccation responses are shared across diverse eukaryotes (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>), and the potential to evolve tolerance is widespread. Desiccation-tolerant plants have the added challenge of withstanding excess light during prolonged desiccation and have evolved unique photoprotective mechanisms such as early light-induced protein gene family expansion in response (<a id="xref-ref-29-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). The origin of desiccation tolerance in plants is likely linked to the colonization of land, where the algal ancestors of plants would experience rapid and prolonged drying. It was previously hypothesized that this tolerance was subsequently lost in vegetative tissues of most land plants, but retained in seeds, spores, and pollen. Vegetative desiccation tolerance was likely regained independently multiple times in angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-5-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). The alternative explanation for the distribution of vegetative desiccation tolerance among angiosperms is multiple reversions in desiccation-sensitive lineages similar to the pattern previously described for the evolution of nitrogen-fixing symbiosis (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). However, multiple independent gains of vegetative desiccation tolerance are more likely due to the large number of reversions required to explain the trait’s distribution. In addition, genomic evidence of independent gains of involved components, including the apparent independent expansion of early light-induced proteins in desiccation-tolerant lineages, suggests multiple independent gains of the desiccation-tolerant phenotype (<a id="xref-ref-29-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). The recurrent evolution of vegetative desiccation tolerance across diverse flowering plants has previously been attributed to rewiring of seed and pollen desiccation pathways (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). Studies examining gene expression during desiccation have repeatedly supported this claim by identifying increased expression of seed-related genes in vegetative tissues during drying (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>, <a id="xref-ref-8-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). However, few studies have conducted genome-wide comparisons of tolerant and sensitive species to test whether these seed-related genes are truly unique to desiccation-tolerant plants. Another possibility is that these broad seed-related pathways were never lost in vegetative tissues, but were instead repurposed for roles in typical drought responses.</p><p id="p-34">Using a comparative approach, we observed a similar pattern of seed-related pathway expression under water deficit in desiccation-tolerant and -sensitive grasses. This is not totally surprising as some seed-dehydration–associated pathways have well-characterized overlap with drought responses such as accumulation of osmoprotectants, LEA proteins, and ROS scavengers (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a><a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-42"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>). For example, 22 of the 57 LEA genes in <em>Arabidopsis</em> have drought-induced expression but only 10 LEA genes have overlapping expression in drying seeds (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). Across desiccation-tolerant and -sensitive grasses, we observed a substantial overlap between LEA expression under water deficit in leaves and developing seeds. More broadly, ABA is a central signaling molecule for environmental stress and seed development, and ABA-based regulatory networks have some overlap between these processes (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). The ABA-responsive transcription factor ABI3 is a well-characterized regulator of seed maturation drying pathways (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>, <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). In the desiccation-tolerant monocot <em>Xerophyta viscosa</em>, orthologs to the majority of ABI3-regulated genes are expressed in leaves during dehydration (<a id="xref-ref-7-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). Similarly, we found that most of the 23 orthogroups containing ABI3-responsive genes were up-regulated during desiccation in both <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>. However, many of these orthogroups were also up-regulated during water-deficit stress in the desiccation-sensitive grasses <em>E. tef</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>. Although most ABI3-responsive genes are induced under water deficit, expression of ABI3 orthologs was low in both <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em>. This is consistent with recent findings from <em>Xerophyta</em> <em>humilis</em>, where there was no evidence that ABI3 or the canonical LAFL seed maturation regulatory network was responsible for desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). Taken together, this suggests that many “seed-related” genes are expressed as a universal response to water deficit, but the regulation of these processes is likely distinct from seed regulatory networks.</p><p id="p-35">We identified a strong correlation between the induction of seed-related genes and the severity of the drought treatment in grasses. Comparatively few seed-related genes were expressed under mild drought in any grass, but the number rose dramatically as relative water content decreased. This trend may have been overlooked in previous studies as most water-deficit experiments are comparatively mild, and few survey lethal or sublethal stresses. These seed-related pathways may be induced as a last ditch effort under severe conditions, but are insufficient or too late to prevent fatal damage. In developing seeds, water content decreases as the accumulation of food reserves drives out cellular water (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). Seeds begin to acquire desiccation tolerance starting at ∼50% RWC, and 50% of seeds were tolerant at ∼30% RWC (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>). This indicates that a generic water-deficit response occurs in seeds prior to a desiccation response. This pattern is very similar to what is observed in dehydrating resurrection species and suggestive of a common ancestral mechanism related to water content or water potential. This hypothesis warrants further consideration, as only two datasets from desiccation-sensitive grasses with coupled expression and drought physiology data have been collected. Further work comparing the expression of otherwise seed-specific genes during severe drought, and, importantly, captured at low relative water contents, is needed.</p><p id="p-36">Our results suggest a strong overlap between drought and desiccation responses, but what elements are unique to desiccation? It was previously shown that expansion of ELIP genes is conserved among all sequenced desiccation-tolerant plants (<a id="xref-ref-29-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Similar to other desiccation-tolerant plants, <em>E. nindensis</em> contains an expansion of ELIPs, and the majority are highly expressed during dehydration. Interestingly, ELIPs are most highly expressed in <em>E. nindensis</em> during rehydration, in contrast to patterns observed in chlorophyll-retaining species. High ELIP expression was also observed during rehydration in the chlorophyll-degrading species <em>Xerophyta viscosa</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). ELIPs may function to protect leaves during the slow postrehydration recovery in chlorophyll-degrading species, mirroring their role in germinating seeds (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Consistent with adaptation to light stress, <em>E. nindensis</em> expresses genes for anthocyanin and carotenoid biosynthesis at much higher levels than <em>E. tef</em>. Furthermore, orthologs of the hub regulator for photomorphogenesis HY5 are expressed during drought exclusively in the desiccation-tolerant species. Thus, we infer that mechanisms to protect against photooxidative damage are critical for the desiccation-tolerant phenotype even in chlorophyll-degrading species.</p><p id="p-37">Desiccation tolerance is found in five tribes of chloridoid grasses across nine genera within four distinct clades among numerous desiccation-sensitive species (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). Many chloridoid grasses are drought and heat tolerant, and this preadaptation may have facilitated the recurrent evolution of desiccation tolerance within this subfamily. It is possible that some desiccation tolerance mechanisms are shared with desiccation-sensitive but still highly resilient members of this subfamily. This could explain the strong overlap in expression patterns between <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>E. tef</em> and the induction of similar seed-related genes. Species like <em>E. tef</em> may represent evolutionary intermediates with induction of some desiccation-related pathways that are not observed in less tolerant plants. Desiccation tolerance strategies vary within grasses, and <em>E. nindensis</em> and <em>O. thomaeum</em> utilize different photoprotective strategies to cope with anhydrobiosis. <em>E. nindensis</em> largely degrades its chlorophyll prior to desiccation, and <em>O. thomaeum</em> retains and protects its chlorophyll and photosystem II complexes. Although many desiccation-specific orthogroups are similarly expressed in both species, we observed unique expression patterns that may reflect differing photoprotective strategies.</p><p id="p-38">Chromatin remodeling plays an important role in seed development, and it was previously proposed that chromatin dynamics could have a similar role in desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>, <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>, <a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). We found no global changes in methylation during desiccation; however, we did observe an intriguing pattern of enrichment of H3K4me3 histone modifications upstream of genes down-regulated during desiccation. Typically, H3K4me3 histone marks accumulate upstream of actively transcribed genes, a pattern we also observed in both well-watered and desiccated samples. However, the observed enrichment near down-regulated genes is unusual. In seeds, chromatin remodeling is important for genome compaction as well as for preparing the genome for transcription upon germination (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). We hypothesize that the observed enrichment of H3K4me3 histone marks upstream of down-regulated genes may be a sign of chromatin remodeling in preparation for transcription upon rehydration.</p><p id="p-39">Here, we propose that seed dehydration pathways are important components of both drought and vegetative desiccation responses. Numerous previous studies have shown that seed dehydration genes are important in protecting leaves of desiccation-tolerant species. We identified a similar pattern in <em>E. nindensis</em>; however, comparisons to <em>E. tef</em> and other grasses reveal that seed pathways are also important in leaves of desiccation-sensitive species during drought. The importance of these pathways for general drought response has been understated in the previous literature. Nevertheless, some aspects of seed dehydration pathways do appear to be specific to seeds and leaves of desiccation-tolerant plants. Photoprotective pathways that are important in germinating seeds are also active in desiccated and rehydrating leaf tissue of resurrection plants. The timely, coordinated, and orderly induction of these desiccation-responsive pathways may be essential for engineering improved stress resilience in crop plants.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><p id="p-40" class="flushleft">Accessions of <em>E. nindensis</em> (PI 410063) and <em>E. tef</em> (PI 524434) were obtained from the US Department of Agriculture Germplasm Resources Information Network (<a href="http://www.ars-grin.gov/" class="in-nw" target="_blank">http://www.ars-grin.gov/</a>). Methodological details of plant growth conditions, water-deficit treatments, nucleic acid isolation, genome assembly, comparative genomics, expression analysis, Bisulfite-Seq, and ChIP-seq are described in <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.2001928117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplemental Materials and Methods</em></a>.</p><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Data Availability.</h3><p id="p-41">The raw PacBio data, Illumina DNA-seq, RNA-seq data, Bisulfite-seq, and ChIP-seq are available from the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Short Read Archive. <em>E. nindensis</em> RNAseq data can be found under BioProject accession no. PRJNA548129 and PRJNA548367, and <em>E. tef</em> RNAseq data can be found under BioProject accession no. PRJNA548000. Bisulfite-seq and ChIP-seq for <em>E. nindensis </em>can be found under BioProject acession no. PRJNA548367. The <em>E. nindensis</em> V2.1 genome can be downloaded from NCBI BioProject accession no. PRJNA622516 and CoGe (ID 54689). Code used to analyze the expression data is available on GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/pardojer23/VanBuren_Lab_Genomics_Tools" class="in-nw" target="_blank">https://github.com/pardojer23/VanBuren_Lab_Genomics_Tools</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-42">We thank Yao Cao for help with DAPI staining and karyotyping, Alan Yocca for assistance with his Ka/Ks pipeline, and Scott Pardo for reviewing the reporting of statistical results.
